        "title": "7 Things to Know About Spacewalks",
        "body": "<p><a class=\"tumblr_blog\" href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/post/132038157669\">nasa</a>:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>On Wednesday, Oct. 28 and Friday, Nov. 6, Commander Scott Kelly and Flight Engineer Kjell Lindgren will perform <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/press-release/nasa-tv-to-air-briefing-on-two-upcoming-spacewalks-by-us-astronauts\">spacewalks</a> in support of space station assembly and maintenance. You can watch both of these events live on NASA Television. But, before you do, here are 7 <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/audience/foreducators/spacesuits/home/clickable_suit_nf.html#.Vi_YPK6rSu4\">things to know</a>:</p>\n<p><b>1. What’s the Point of a Spacewalk?</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/798e628716aec15a82b15ccb055366b4/tumblr_inline_nww1x8CJlQ1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/audience/forstudents/k-4/stories/nasa-knows/what-is-a-spacewalk-k4.html\">Spacewalks</a> are important events where crew members repair, maintain and upgrade parts of the International Space Station. Spacewalks can also be referred to as an EVA – Extravehicular Activity. On Wednesday, Oct. 28, Commander Scott Kelly and Flight Engineer Kjell Lindgren will complete a <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/press-release/nasa-tv-to-air-briefing-on-two-upcoming-spacewalks-by-us-astronauts\">spacewalk</a>. During this time they will service the Canadarm2 robotic arm, route cables for a future docking port, and place a thermal cover over a <a href=\"http://ams.nasa.gov/\">dark matter detection experiment</a>, which is a state-of-the-art particles physics detector that has been attached to the station since 2011. </p>\n<p><b>2. What Do They Wear?</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"900\" data-orig-height=\"506\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/ac16d2f75d6a07b5a55bce17168d57c3/tumblr_inline_nww25189PR1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"900\" data-orig-height=\"506\"/></figure><p>The <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/externalflash/ISSRG/pdfs/emu.pdf\">Extravehicular Mobility Unit (EMU)</a> spacewalking suit weighs around 350 pounds. It’s weightless in space, but mass is still very real. The EMU provides a crew member with life support and an enclosure that enables them to work outside the space station. The suit provides atmospheric containment, thermal insulation, cooling, solar radiation protection and micrometeoroid/orbital debris protection. </p>\n<p><b>3. How Long Are Spacewalks? </b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/94fb3bf3dd05291abfb0ec62dd5473e4/tumblr_inline_nwwa0tzPo81tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Spacewalks typically last around 6 ½ hours, but can be extended to 7 or 8 hours, if necessary. The timeline is designed to accommodate as many tasks as possible, as spacewalks require an enormous amount of work to prepare. </p>\n<p><b>4. What About Eating and Drinking?</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"4288\" data-orig-height=\"2848\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/55d8ffb0be583149dff3bac9493981a0/tumblr_inline_nwwb92kJjU1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"4288\" data-orig-height=\"2848\"/></figure><p>Before a spacewalk astronauts eat light, usually something like a protein bar. The spacesuits also have a drink bag inside, and there is a bite valve that allows ready access to water. </p>\n<p><b>5. What About Communication?</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/6d6679e38a52ec31712cd4c0e2f353ac/tumblr_inline_nwwapoBrmj1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Spacewalkers wear a ‘comm’ cap that allows them to constantly communicate with astronauts inside the space station that are helping with the walk, and with mission control. Astronauts also wear a checklist on their left wrist called a “cuff checklist”. This list contains emergency procedures. </p>\n<p><b>6. What About Light?</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/63dffab4bdd2a9035cd834222c2ab04a/tumblr_inline_nwwaagqG021tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Something that most people don’t realize about spacewalks is that the crew will experience a sunrise/sunset every 45 minutes. Luckily, their spacesuits are equipped with lights that allow them to see in times of darkness.</p>\n<p><b>7. How Do They Stay Safe?</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"500\" data-orig-height=\"498\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/a69a0bde3887b4b8ad24fe539e0f4894/tumblr_inline_nww3leQ0nr1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"500\" data-orig-height=\"498\"/></figure><p>When on a spacewalk, astronauts use safety tethers to stay close to their spacecraft. One end of the tether is hooked to the spacewalker, while the other end is connected to the vehicle. Another way astronauts stay safe is by wearing a SAFER, which is a Simplified Aid for EVA Rescue. This device is worn like a backpack and uses small jet thrusters to let an astronaut move around in space. </p>\n<p><b>You can watch both of the upcoming spacewalks live on: <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/multimedia/nasatv/index.html#media\">NASA Television</a> or the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/centers/ames/iphone/index.html\">NASA App</a>, or follow along on <a href=\"https://twitter.com/Space_Station\">@Space_Station Twitter</a>.</b></p>\n<p>Wednesday, Oct. 28: Coverage begins at 6:30 a.m. EDT. Spacewalk begins at 8:10 a.m.</p>\n<p>Friday, Nov. 6: Coverage begins at 5:45 a.m. EDT. Spacewalk begins at 7:15 a.m.</p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a> <br/></p>\n</blockquote>",

The suit provides atmospheric containment, thermal insulation, cooling, solar radiation protection and micrometeoroid/orbital debris protection. </p>\n<p><b>3. How Long Are Spacewalks? </b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/94fb3bf3dd05291abfb0ec62dd5473e4/tumblr_inline_nwwa0tzPo81tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>Spacewalks typically last around 6 ½ hours, but can be extended to 7 or 8 hours, if necessary. The timeline is designed to accommodate as many tasks as possible, as spacewalks require an enormous amount of work to prepare. </p>\n<p><b>4. What About Eating and Drinking?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/55d8ffb0be583149dff3bac9493981a0/tumblr_inline_nwwb92kJjU1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>Before a spacewalk astronauts eat light, usually something like a protein bar. The spacesuits also have a drink bag inside, and there is a bite valve that allows ready access to water. </p>\n<p><b>5. What About Communication?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/6d6679e38a52ec31712cd4c0e2f353ac/tumblr_inline_nwwapoBrmj1tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>Spacewalkers wear a ‘comm’ cap that allows them to constantly communicate with astronauts inside the space station that are helping with the walk, and with mission control. Astronauts also wear a checklist on their left wrist called a “cuff checklist”. This list contains emergency procedures. </p>\n<p><b>6. What About Light?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/63dffab4bdd2a9035cd834222c2ab04a/tumblr_inline_nwwaagqG021tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>Something that most people don’t realize about spacewalks is that the crew will experience a sunrise/sunset every 45 minutes. Luckily, their spacesuits are equipped with lights that allow them to see in times of darkness.</p>\n<p><b>7. How Do They Stay Safe?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/a69a0bde3887b4b8ad24fe539e0f4894/tumblr_inline_nww3leQ0nr1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>When on a spacewalk, astronauts use safety tethers to stay close to their spacecraft. One end of the tether is hooked to the spacewalker, while the other end is connected to the vehicle. Another way astronauts stay safe is by wearing a SAFER, which is a Simplified Aid for EVA Rescue. This device is worn like a backpack and uses small jet thrusters to let an astronaut move around in space. </p>\n<p><b>You can watch both of the upcoming spacewalks live on: <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/multimedia/nasatv/index.html#media\">NASA Television</a> or the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/centers/ames/iphone/index.html\">NASA App</a>, or follow along on <a href=\"https://twitter.com/Space_Station\">@Space_Station Twitter</a>.</b></p>\n<p>Wednesday, Oct. 28: Coverage begins at 6:30 a.m. EDT. Spacewalk begins at 8:10 a.m.</p>\n<p>Friday, Nov. 6: Coverage begins at 5:45 a.m. EDT. Spacewalk begins at 7:15 a.m.</p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a> <br /></p>",

            "content_raw": "<p>The best part of the trailer for Adam Sandler’s upcoming Netflix movie, <i>The Ridiculous 6</i>, happens in the first four seconds, when the only thing on the screen is a horse’s legs. It’s pretty much downhill from there.</p><p>It’s difficult to accurately isolate what makes this trailer (and, by extension, the movie) so terrible, because it’s all one idiotic, racist, masturbatory, incoherent, probably very expensive piece of garbage. The plot involves hackneyed imaginings of cowboys and Native Americans, but it’s hard to decipher any cohesive narrative over the circus music, gratuitous screaming, and awful, unplaceable accents.</p><p><i>MY OPINION: THIS IS BAD</i></p><p>There are a baffling — BAFFLING — number of famous actors who, against all logical reasoning, apparently agreed to be in this movie. <a href=\"http://blogs.indiewire.com/theplaylist/watch-first-trailer-for-adam-sandlers-controversial-comedy-the-ridiculous-6-20151027\"><i>Indiewire</i> lists <i>19</i></a><i> — NINETEEN —</i>famous men (including Steve Buscemi, Rob Schneider, Dan Aykroyd, Vanilla Ice, and Will Forte) plus Whitney Cummings, who appears to be the only woman in the movie save for a few extras.</p><p>Maybe Netflix is expanding its offerings to include terrible movies for a reason, or maybe some Netflix exec just really likes Adam Sandler. In any case, you don’t need to watch this trailer. Really, you could do anything else, and your day will be better than it would be if you watched this trailer.</p><p><i>— Lizzie Plaugic for The Verge</i></p><p><b><i>Note: <a href=\"http://www.theverge.com/2015/10/27/9622086/adam-sandler-ridiculous-6-trailer-netflix?utm_medium=social&amp;utm_source=tumblr&amp;utm_campaign=theverge\">the trailer</a> for The Ridiculous 6 is not embedded in this post because it’s bad. But watch it if you absolutely must.</i></b></p>",
            "content": "<p>The best part of the trailer for Adam Sandler’s upcoming Netflix movie, <i>The Ridiculous 6</i>, happens in the first four seconds, when the only thing on the screen is a horse’s legs. It’s pretty much downhill from there.</p><p>It’s difficult to accurately isolate what makes this trailer (and, by extension, the movie) so terrible, because it’s all one idiotic, racist, masturbatory, incoherent, probably very expensive piece of garbage. The plot involves hackneyed imaginings of cowboys and Native Americans, but it’s hard to decipher any cohesive narrative over the circus music, gratuitous screaming, and awful, unplaceable accents.</p><p><i>MY OPINION: THIS IS BAD</i></p><p>There are a baffling — BAFFLING — number of famous actors who, against all logical reasoning, apparently agreed to be in this movie. <a href=\"http://blogs.indiewire.com/theplaylist/watch-first-trailer-for-adam-sandlers-controversial-comedy-the-ridiculous-6-20151027\"><i>Indiewire</i> lists <i>19</i></a><i> — NINETEEN —</i>famous men (including Steve Buscemi, Rob Schneider, Dan Aykroyd, Vanilla Ice, and Will Forte) plus Whitney Cummings, who appears to be the only woman in the movie save for a few extras.</p><p>Maybe Netflix is expanding its offerings to include terrible movies for a reason, or maybe some Netflix exec just really likes Adam Sandler. In any case, you don’t need to watch this trailer. Really, you could do anything else, and your day will be better than it would be if you watched this trailer.</p><p><i>— Lizzie Plaugic for The Verge</i></p><p><b><i>Note: <a href=\"http://www.theverge.com/2015/10/27/9622086/adam-sandler-ridiculous-6-trailer-netflix?utm_medium=social&amp;utm_source=tumblr&amp;utm_campaign=theverge\">the trailer</a> for The Ridiculous 6 is not embedded in this post because it’s bad. But watch it if you absolutely must.</i></b></p>",

      {
        "blog_name": "theverge",
        "id": 131660099513,
        "post_url": "http://theverge.tumblr.com/post/131660099513/nasas-fleet-of-planet-hunters-and-world-explorers",
        "slug": "nasas-fleet-of-planet-hunters-and-world-explorers",
        "type": "text",
        "date": "2015-10-22 02:38:36 GMT",
        "timestamp": 1445481516,
        "state": "published",
        "format": "html",
        "reblog_key": "alUJS34z",
        "tags": [],
        "short_url": "http://tmblr.co/ZP0mpw1wdZQ_v",
        "summary": "NASA’s Fleet of Planet-hunters and World-explorers",
        "recommended_source": null,
        "recommended_color": null,
        "followed": false,
        "highlighted": [],
        "liked": false,
        "note_count": 1441,
        "title": "NASA’s Fleet of Planet-hunters and World-explorers",
        "body": "<p><a class=\"tumblr_blog\" href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/post/131493842134\">nasa</a>:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Around every star is at least one planet, so we’re bound to find one that is rocky, like Earth, and possibly suitable for life. While we’re not quite to the point where we can zoom up and take clear snapshots of the thousands of distant worlds we’ve found outside our solar system, there are ways we can figure out what exoplanets light years away are made of, and if they have signs of basic building blocks for life. Here are a few current and upcoming missions helping us explore new worlds:</p>\n<p><b>Kepler</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"540\" data-orig-height=\"360\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/9f8b5731f74aa6982550518dcc7db838/tumblr_inline_nwbpahC27f1tzhl5u_540.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"540\" data-orig-height=\"360\"/></figure><p>Launched in 2009, the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/kepler\">Kepler</a> space telescope searched for planets by looking for telltale dips in a star’s brightness caused by crossing, or transiting, planets. It has confirmed more than 1,000 planets; of these, fewer than 20 are Earth-size (therefore possibly rocky) and in the habitable zone – the area around a star where liquid water could pool on the surface of an orbiting planet. Astronomers using Kepler data found the <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/ames/kepler/nasas-kepler-discovers-first-earth-size-planet-in-the-habitable-zone-of-another-star\">first Earth-sized planet orbiting in the habitable zone of its star</a>.</p>\n<p>In May 2013, a second pointing wheel on the spacecraft broke, making it not stable enough to continue its original mission. But clever engineers and scientists got to work, and in May 2014, Kepler took on a new job as the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/kepler/keplers-second-light-how-k2-will-work\">K2 mission</a>. K2 continues the search for other worlds but has introduced new opportunities to observe star clusters, young and old stars, active galaxies and supernovae.</p>\n<p><b>Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/a6da68cc409df02022a2a0d99b28bd7f/tumblr_inline_nwbpy4oJO41tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Revving up for launch around 2017-2018, NASA’s <a href=\"http://tess.gsfc.nasa.gov/\">Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)</a> will find new planets the same way Kepler does, but right in the stellar backyard of our solar system while covering 400 times the sky area. It plans to monitor 200,000 bright, nearby stars for planets, with a focus on finding Earth and Super-Earth-sized planets. </p>\n<p>Once we’ve narrowed down the best targets for follow-up, astronomers can figure out what these planets are made of, and what’s in the atmosphere. One of the ways to look into the atmosphere is through <a href=\"https://solarsystem.nasa.gov/deepimpact/science/spectroscopy.cfm\">spectroscopy</a>.  </p>\n<p>As a planet passes between us and its star, a small amount of starlight is absorbed by the gas in the planet’s atmosphere. This leaves telltale chemical “fingerprints” in the star’s light that astronomers can use to discover the chemical composition of the atmosphere, such as methane, carbon dioxide, or water vapor. <br/></p>\n<p><b>James Webb Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/0926fb2ae689f6740358c7f274ebb6a9/tumblr_inline_nwbps29rMm1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Launching in 2018, NASA’s most powerful telescope to date, the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/webb/main/index.html\">James Webb Space Telescope (JWST)</a>, will not only be able to search for planets orbiting distant stars, its near-infrared multi-object spectrograph will split infrared light into its different colors- spectrum- providing scientists with information about an physical properties about an exoplanet’s atmosphere, including temperature, mass, and chemical composition. <br/></p>\n<p><b>Hubble Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/8adac179542571abb5284eaba414544a/tumblr_inline_nwbqh6Z1Jz1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/hubble/main/index.html\">Hubble Space Telescope</a> is better than ever after 25 years of science, and has found evidence for <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/feature/goddard/hubble-sees-a-behemoth-bleeding-atmosphere-around-a-warm-exoplanet\">atmospheres bleeding off exoplanets</a> very close to their stars, and even provided <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press/2014/october/nasas-hubble-maps-the-temperature-and-water-vapor-on-an-extreme-exoplanet\">thermal maps of exoplanet atmospheres</a>. Hubble holds the record for finding the <a href=\"http://hubblesite.org/newscenter/archive/releases/star/extrasolar-planets/2006/34/results/100/\">farthest exoplanets discovered to date</a>, located 26,000 light-years away in the hub of our Milky Way galaxy.<br/></p>\n<p><b>Chandra X-ray Observatory</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/d76a207907a8385596aa78b62f9ccdef/tumblr_inline_nwbreqBBpW1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p><a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/chandra/main/index.html\">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a> can detect exoplanets passing in front of their parent stars. X-ray observations can also help give clues on an exoplanet’s atmosphere and magnetic fields. It has observed an exoplanet that <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press/2014/september/nasa-s-chandra-x-ray-observatory-finds-planet-that-makes-star-act-deceptively/#.ViUF1RCrT9A\">made its star act much older than it actually is</a>. <br/></p>\n<p><b>Spitzer Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/38eb629884e3f3c0f6c28a85e0779365/tumblr_inline_nwh1m3WKUn1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/spitzer/main/index.html\">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> has been unveiling hidden cosmic objects with its dust-piercing infrared vision for more than 12 years. It helped pioneer the study of atmospheres and weather on large, gaseous exoplanets. Spitzer can help narrow down the sizes of exoplanets, and recently confirmed <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press-release/nasas-spitzer-confirms-closest-rocky-exoplanet\">the closest known rocky planet to Earth</a>.<br/></p>\n<p><b>SOFIA</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/13a482a9eea24b686bea862bcda46f19/tumblr_inline_nwbqqi1JKN1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>The <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/SOFIA/index.html\">Stratospheric Observatory for Infrared Astronomy (SOFIA)</a> is an airplane mounted with an infrared telescope that can fly above more than 99 percent of Earth’s atmospheric water vapor. Unlike most space observatories, SOFIA can be routinely upgraded and repaired. It can look at planetary-forming systems and has recently observed its first <a href=\"https://www.sofia.usra.edu/Gallery/science/SCI2015_0004.html\">exoplanet transit</a>. <br/></p>\n<p><b>What’s Coming Next?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-embed tmblr-full\" data-provider=\"youtube\" data-orig-width=\"540\" data-orig-height=\"304\" data-url=\"https%3A%2F%2Fwww.youtube.com%2Fwatch%3Fv%3DczD5YcR1G4M\"><iframe width=\"540\" height=\"304\" id=\"youtube_iframe\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/czD5YcR1G4M?feature=oembed&amp;enablejsapi=1&amp;origin=https://safe.txmblr.com&amp;wmode=opaque\" frameborder=\"0\"></iframe></figure><p>Analyzing the chemical makeup of Earth-sized, rocky planets with thin atmospheres is a big challenge, since smaller planets are incredibly faint compared to their stars. One solution is to block the light of the planets’ glaring stars so that we can directly see the reflected light of the planets. Telescope instruments called coronagraphs use masks to block the starlight while letting the planet’s light pass through. Another possible tool is a large, flower-shaped structure known as the <a href=\"http://planetquest.jpl.nasa.gov/video/15\">starshade</a>. This structure would fly in tandem <a href=\"http://wfirst.gsfc.nasa.gov/about/\">with a space telescope</a> to block the light of a star before it enters the telescope. <br/></p>\n<p><i>All images (except SOFIA) are artist illustrations.</i></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br/></p>\n</blockquote>",

          "tree_html": "<p><a class=\"tumblr_blog\" href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/post/131493842134\">nasa</a>:</p><blockquote>\n<p>Around every star is at least one planet, so we’re bound to find one that is rocky, like Earth, and possibly suitable for life. While we’re not quite to the point where we can zoom up and take clear snapshots of the thousands of distant worlds we’ve found outside our solar system, there are ways we can figure out what exoplanets light years away are made of, and if they have signs of basic building blocks for life. Here are a few current and upcoming missions helping us explore new worlds:</p>\n<p><b>Kepler</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"540\" data-orig-height=\"360\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/9f8b5731f74aa6982550518dcc7db838/tumblr_inline_nwbpahC27f1tzhl5u_540.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"540\" data-orig-height=\"360\"></figure><p>Launched in 2009, the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/kepler\">Kepler</a> space telescope searched for planets by looking for telltale dips in a star’s brightness caused by crossing, or transiting, planets. It has confirmed more than 1,000 planets; of these, fewer than 20 are Earth-size (therefore possibly rocky) and in the habitable zone – the area around a star where liquid water could pool on the surface of an orbiting planet. Astronomers using Kepler data found the <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/ames/kepler/nasas-kepler-discovers-first-earth-size-planet-in-the-habitable-zone-of-another-star\">first Earth-sized planet orbiting in the habitable zone of its star</a>.</p>\n<p>In May 2013, a second pointing wheel on the spacecraft broke, making it not stable enough to continue its original mission. But clever engineers and scientists got to work, and in May 2014, Kepler took on a new job as the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/kepler/keplers-second-light-how-k2-will-work\">K2 mission</a>. K2 continues the search for other worlds but has introduced new opportunities to observe star clusters, young and old stars, active galaxies and supernovae.</p>\n<p><b>Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/a6da68cc409df02022a2a0d99b28bd7f/tumblr_inline_nwbpy4oJO41tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>Revving up for launch around 2017-2018, NASA’s <a href=\"http://tess.gsfc.nasa.gov/\">Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)</a> will find new planets the same way Kepler does, but right in the stellar backyard of our solar system while covering 400 times the sky area. It plans to monitor 200,000 bright, nearby stars for planets, with a focus on finding Earth and Super-Earth-sized planets. </p>\n<p>Once we’ve narrowed down the best targets for follow-up, astronomers can figure out what these planets are made of, and what’s in the atmosphere. One of the ways to look into the atmosphere is through <a href=\"https://solarsystem.nasa.gov/deepimpact/science/spectroscopy.cfm\">spectroscopy</a>.  </p>\n<p>As a planet passes between us and its star, a small amount of starlight is absorbed by the gas in the planet’s atmosphere. This leaves telltale chemical “fingerprints” in the star’s light that astronomers can use to discover the chemical composition of the atmosphere, such as methane, carbon dioxide, or water vapor. <br></p>\n<p><b>James Webb Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/0926fb2ae689f6740358c7f274ebb6a9/tumblr_inline_nwbps29rMm1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>Launching in 2018, NASA’s most powerful telescope to date, the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/webb/main/index.html\">James Webb Space Telescope (JWST)</a>, will not only be able to search for planets orbiting distant stars, its near-infrared multi-object spectrograph will split infrared light into its different colors- spectrum- providing scientists with information about an physical properties about an exoplanet’s atmosphere, including temperature, mass, and chemical composition. <br></p>\n<p><b>Hubble Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/8adac179542571abb5284eaba414544a/tumblr_inline_nwbqh6Z1Jz1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/hubble/main/index.html\">Hubble Space Telescope</a> is better than ever after 25 years of science, and has found evidence for <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/feature/goddard/hubble-sees-a-behemoth-bleeding-atmosphere-around-a-warm-exoplanet\">atmospheres bleeding off exoplanets</a> very close to their stars, and even provided <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press/2014/october/nasas-hubble-maps-the-temperature-and-water-vapor-on-an-extreme-exoplanet\">thermal maps of exoplanet atmospheres</a>. Hubble holds the record for finding the <a href=\"http://hubblesite.org/newscenter/archive/releases/star/extrasolar-planets/2006/34/results/100/\">farthest exoplanets discovered to date</a>, located 26,000 light-years away in the hub of our Milky Way galaxy.<br></p>\n<p><b>Chandra X-ray Observatory</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/d76a207907a8385596aa78b62f9ccdef/tumblr_inline_nwbreqBBpW1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p><a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/chandra/main/index.html\">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a> can detect exoplanets passing in front of their parent stars. X-ray observations can also help give clues on an exoplanet’s atmosphere and magnetic fields. It has observed an exoplanet that <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press/2014/september/nasa-s-chandra-x-ray-observatory-finds-planet-that-makes-star-act-deceptively/#.ViUF1RCrT9A\">made its star act much older than it actually is</a>. <br></p>\n<p><b>Spitzer Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/38eb629884e3f3c0f6c28a85e0779365/tumblr_inline_nwh1m3WKUn1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/spitzer/main/index.html\">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> has been unveiling hidden cosmic objects with its dust-piercing infrared vision for more than 12 years. It helped pioneer the study of atmospheres and weather on large, gaseous exoplanets. Spitzer can help narrow down the sizes of exoplanets, and recently confirmed <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press-release/nasas-spitzer-confirms-closest-rocky-exoplanet\">the closest known rocky planet to Earth</a>.<br></p>\n<p><b>SOFIA</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/13a482a9eea24b686bea862bcda46f19/tumblr_inline_nwbqqi1JKN1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>The <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/SOFIA/index.html\">Stratospheric Observatory for Infrared Astronomy (SOFIA)</a> is an airplane mounted with an infrared telescope that can fly above more than 99 percent of Earth’s atmospheric water vapor. Unlike most space observatories, SOFIA can be routinely upgraded and repaired. It can look at planetary-forming systems and has recently observed its first <a href=\"https://www.sofia.usra.edu/Gallery/science/SCI2015_0004.html\">exoplanet transit</a>. <br></p>\n<p><b>What’s Coming Next?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-embed tmblr-full\" data-provider=\"youtube\" data-orig-width=\"540\" data-orig-height=\"304\" data-url=\"https%3A%2F%2Fwww.youtube.com%2Fwatch%3Fv%3DczD5YcR1G4M\"><iframe width=\"540\" height=\"304\" id=\"youtube_iframe\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/czD5YcR1G4M?feature=oembed&amp;enablejsapi=1&amp;origin=https://safe.txmblr.com&amp;wmode=opaque\" frameborder=\"0\"></iframe></figure><p>Analyzing the chemical makeup of Earth-sized, rocky planets with thin atmospheres is a big challenge, since smaller planets are incredibly faint compared to their stars. One solution is to block the light of the planets’ glaring stars so that we can directly see the reflected light of the planets. Telescope instruments called coronagraphs use masks to block the starlight while letting the planet’s light pass through. Another possible tool is a large, flower-shaped structure known as the <a href=\"http://planetquest.jpl.nasa.gov/video/15\">starshade</a>. This structure would fly in tandem <a href=\"http://wfirst.gsfc.nasa.gov/about/\">with a space telescope</a> to block the light of a star before it enters the telescope. <br></p>\n<p><i>All images (except SOFIA) are artist illustrations.</i></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br></p>\n</blockquote>",

            "content": "<p>Around every star is at least one planet, so we’re bound to find one that is rocky, like Earth, and possibly suitable for life. While we’re not quite to the point where we can zoom up and take clear snapshots of the thousands of distant worlds we’ve found outside our solar system, there are ways we can figure out what exoplanets light years away are made of, and if they have signs of basic building blocks for life. Astronomers using Kepler data found the <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/ames/kepler/nasas-kepler-discovers-first-earth-size-planet-in-the-habitable-zone-of-another-star\">first Earth-sized planet orbiting in the habitable zone of its star</a>.</p>\n<p>In May 2013, a second pointing wheel on the spacecraft broke, making it not stable enough to continue its original mission. But clever engineers and scientists got to work, and in May 2014, Kepler took on a new job as the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/kepler/keplers-second-light-how-k2-will-work\">K2 mission</a>. K2 continues the search for other worlds but has introduced new opportunities to observe star clusters, young and old stars, active galaxies and supernovae.</p>\n<p><b>Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/a6da68cc409df02022a2a0d99b28bd7f/tumblr_inline_nwbpy4oJO41tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>Revving up for launch around 2017-2018, NASA’s <a href=\"http://tess.gsfc.nasa.gov/\">Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)</a> will find new planets the same way Kepler does, but right in the stellar backyard of our solar system while covering 400 times the sky area. It plans to monitor 200,000 bright, nearby stars for planets, with a focus on finding Earth and Super-Earth-sized planets. </p>\n<p>Once we’ve narrowed down the best targets for follow-up, astronomers can figure out what these planets are made of, and what’s in the atmosphere. One of the ways to look into the atmosphere is through <a href=\"https://solarsystem.nasa.gov/deepimpact/science/spectroscopy.cfm\">spectroscopy</a>.  </p>\n<p>As a planet passes between us and its star, a small amount of starlight is absorbed by the gas in the planet’s atmosphere. This leaves telltale chemical “fingerprints” in the star’s light that astronomers can use to discover the chemical composition of the atmosphere, such as methane, carbon dioxide, or water vapor. <br /></p>\n<p><b>James Webb Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/0926fb2ae689f6740358c7f274ebb6a9/tumblr_inline_nwbps29rMm1tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>Launching in 2018, NASA’s most powerful telescope to date, the <a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/webb/main/index.html\">James Webb Space Telescope (JWST)</a>, will not only be able to search for planets orbiting distant stars, its near-infrared multi-object spectrograph will split infrared light into its different colors- spectrum- providing scientists with information about an physical properties about an exoplanet’s atmosphere, including temperature, mass, and chemical composition. <br /></p>\n<p><b>Hubble Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/8adac179542571abb5284eaba414544a/tumblr_inline_nwbqh6Z1Jz1tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/hubble/main/index.html\">Hubble Space Telescope</a> is better than ever after 25 years of science, and has found evidence for <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/feature/goddard/hubble-sees-a-behemoth-bleeding-atmosphere-around-a-warm-exoplanet\">atmospheres bleeding off exoplanets</a> very close to their stars, and even provided <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press/2014/october/nasas-hubble-maps-the-temperature-and-water-vapor-on-an-extreme-exoplanet\">thermal maps of exoplanet atmospheres</a>. Hubble holds the record for finding the <a href=\"http://hubblesite.org/newscenter/archive/releases/star/extrasolar-planets/2006/34/results/100/\">farthest exoplanets discovered to date</a>, located 26,000 light-years away in the hub of our Milky Way galaxy.<br /></p>\n<p><b>Chandra X-ray Observatory</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/d76a207907a8385596aa78b62f9ccdef/tumblr_inline_nwbreqBBpW1tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p><a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/chandra/main/index.html\">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a> can detect exoplanets passing in front of their parent stars. X-ray observations can also help give clues on an exoplanet’s atmosphere and magnetic fields. It has observed an exoplanet that <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press/2014/september/nasa-s-chandra-x-ray-observatory-finds-planet-that-makes-star-act-deceptively/#.ViUF1RCrT9A\">made its star act much older than it actually is</a>. <br /></p>\n<p><b>Spitzer Space Telescope</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/38eb629884e3f3c0f6c28a85e0779365/tumblr_inline_nwh1m3WKUn1tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p><a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/spitzer/main/index.html\">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> has been unveiling hidden cosmic objects with its dust-piercing infrared vision for more than 12 years. It helped pioneer the study of atmospheres and weather on large, gaseous exoplanets. Spitzer can help narrow down the sizes of exoplanets, and recently confirmed <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/press-release/nasas-spitzer-confirms-closest-rocky-exoplanet\">the closest known rocky planet to Earth</a>.<br /></p>\n<p><b>SOFIA</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/13a482a9eea24b686bea862bcda46f19/tumblr_inline_nwbqqi1JKN1tzhl5u_500.gif\" class=\"\"/></figure><p>The <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/SOFIA/index.html\">Stratospheric Observatory for Infrared Astronomy (SOFIA)</a> is an airplane mounted with an infrared telescope that can fly above more than 99 percent of Earth’s atmospheric water vapor. Unlike most space observatories, SOFIA can be routinely upgraded and repaired. It can look at planetary-forming systems and has recently observed its first <a href=\"https://www.sofia.usra.edu/Gallery/science/SCI2015_0004.html\">exoplanet transit</a>. <br /></p>\n<p><b>What’s Coming Next?</b></p>\n<figure class=\"tmblr-embed tmblr-full\"><p><a href=\"#\"><img src=\"http://assets.tumblr.com/images/inline_placeholder.png\" width=\"18\" height=\"14\"/></a></p></figure><p>Analyzing the chemical makeup of Earth-sized, rocky planets with thin atmospheres is a big challenge, since smaller planets are incredibly faint compared to their stars. One solution is to block the light of the planets’ glaring stars so that we can directly see the reflected light of the planets. Telescope instruments called coronagraphs use masks to block the starlight while letting the planet’s light pass through. Another possible tool is a large, flower-shaped structure known as the <a href=\"http://planetquest.jpl.nasa.gov/video/15\">starshade</a>. This structure would fly in tandem <a href=\"http://wfirst.gsfc.nasa.gov/about/\">with a space telescope</a> to block the light of a star before it enters the telescope. <br /></p>\n<p><i>All images (except SOFIA) are artist illustrations.</i></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br /></p>",

        "blog_name": "theverge",
        "id": 130989615207,
        "post_url": "http://theverge.tumblr.com/post/130989615207/10-out-of-this-world-nasa-spinoff-technologies",
        "slug": "10-out-of-this-world-nasa-spinoff-technologies",
        "type": "text",
        "date": "2015-10-12 01:52:04 GMT",
        "timestamp": 1444614724,
        "state": "published",
        "format": "html",
        "reblog_key": "jTmbk1F9",
        "tags": [],
        "short_url": "http://tmblr.co/ZP0mpw1v-bkXd",
        "summary": "10 Out of this World NASA Spinoff Technologies",
        "recommended_source": null,
        "recommended_color": null,
        "followed": false,
        "highlighted": [],
        "liked": false,
        "note_count": 2181,
        "title": "10 Out of this World NASA Spinoff Technologies",
        "body": "<p><a class=\"tumblr_blog\" href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/post/130833181554\">nasa</a>:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>What is a spinoff? Great question! A NASA spinoff is a technology, originally developed to meet our mission needs that has been transferred to the public and now provides benefits as a commercial product or service. Basically, we create awesome stuff and then share it with the world. Here’s a list of just a few NASA spinoff technologies (in no particular order): </p>\n<p><b>1. Enriched Baby Food</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"1300\" data-orig-height=\"945\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/4673cedff12b548094a908f3a7df34db/tumblr_inline_nvtaxdjf2c1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"1300\" data-orig-height=\"945\"/></figure><p>While developing life support for Mars missions, NASA-funded researchers discovered a natural source for an omega-3 fatty acid that plays a key role in infant development. The ingredient has since been infused in more than 99% of <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2008/ch_8.html\">infant formula</a> on the market and is helping babies worldwide develop healthy brains, eyes and hearts. </p>\n<p><b>2. Digital Camera Sensors</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"490\" data-orig-height=\"277\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/15fc2651424f65612a728ae5ad2f646f/tumblr_inline_nvtb6u25681tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"490\" data-orig-height=\"277\"/></figure><p>Whether you take pictures and videos with a DSLR camera, phone or even a GoPro, you’re using NASA technology. The <a href=\"http://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2010/cg_3.html\">CMOS active pixel sensor</a> in most digital image-capturing devices was invented when we needed to miniaturize cameras for interplanetary missions. </p>\n<p><b>3. Airplane Wing Designs</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"720\" data-orig-height=\"540\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/24f05bfd8009e9e06440893fe89e75b0/tumblr_inline_nvtbeg25NZ1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"720\" data-orig-height=\"540\"/></figure><p>Did you know that we’re with you when you fly? Key aerodynamic advances made by our researchers - such as the up-turned ends of wings, called “<a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2015/t_2.html\">winglets</a>” - are ubiquitous among modern aircraft and have saved many billions of dollars in fuel costs. </p>\n<p><b>4. Precision GPS</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"464\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/a3ae0be91feea51a3167bbf58792ab36/tumblr_inline_nvtbfwGT7H1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"464\"/></figure><p>Uncorrected GPS data can be off by as much as 15 meters thanks to data errors, drift in satellite clocks and interference from Earth’s atmosphere. One of our software packages developed in the 1990s dials in these locations to within centimeters, enabling highly accurate GPS readings anywhere on the planet. One of our most important contributions to modern society, <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2010/ct_7.html\">precise GPS</a> is used in everything from personal devices and commercial airplanes to self-driving tractors. </p>\n<p><b>5. Memory Foam</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"300\" data-orig-height=\"243\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/ec21d3c52521b39163e2977aaa0ad28b/tumblr_inline_nvtbz1gG8b1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"300\" data-orig-height=\"243\"/></figure><p>Possibly the most widely recognized spinoff, <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2005/ch_6.html\">memory foam</a> was invented by our researchers looking for ways to keep its test pilots and astronauts comfortable as they experienced extreme acceleration. Today, memory foam cushions beds, chairs, couches, car and motorcycle seats, shoes and even football helmets. </p>\n<p><b>6. International Search and Rescue System</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"350\" data-orig-height=\"308\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/9ac625142ced68c5b6a86ce266eae791/tumblr_inline_nvtcdx0eSx1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"350\" data-orig-height=\"308\"/></figure><p>We pioneered the technology now used internationally for <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2012/ps_3.html\">search and rescue operations</a>. When pilots, sailors or other travelers and adventurers are stranded, they can activate a personal locator bacon that uses overhead satellites to relay their call for help and precise location to authorities. </p>\n<p><b>7. Improvements to Truck Aerodynamics</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"1362\" data-orig-height=\"1022\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/3ed08b3ff077ad6f11c1a62c0564001d/tumblr_inline_nvtcyfvNWf1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"1362\" data-orig-height=\"1022\"/></figure><p>Nearly every truck on the road has been shaped by NASA - literally. Agency research in <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2008/t_3.html\">vehicle aerodynamic design</a> led to the curves and contours that help modern big rigs cut through the air with less drag. Our contributions to truck design have greatly reduced fuel consumption, perhaps by as much as 6,800 gallons per year for an average vehicle. </p>\n<p><b>8. Shock Absorbers for Buildings and Bridges</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"493\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/78fb9c082d319b39493373af2e70e76d/tumblr_inline_nvtd8oMjn71tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"493\"/></figure><p><a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2015/ps_2.html\">Shock absorbers</a> originally designed to survive the extreme conditions of space shuttle launches are now bracing hundreds of buildings and bridges in earthquake-prone regions all over the world. None of which have suffered even minor damage during an earthquake. </p>\n<p><b>9. Advanced Water Filtration</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"570\" data-orig-height=\"411\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/88b5702b695c048889103a6e4cf9443c/tumblr_inline_nvtdg86E4x1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"570\" data-orig-height=\"411\"/></figure><p>We have recently discovered sources of water on the moon and Mars, but even so space is still practically a desert for human explorers, and every drop possible must be recycled and reused. A <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2013/cg_1.html\">nanofiber filer</a> devised to purify water in orbit is currently at work on Earth. From devices that supply water to remote villages, to a water bottle that lets hikers and adventurers stay hydrated using streams and lakes, our technology is being utilized. </p>\n<p><b>10. Invisible Braces</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"405\" data-orig-height=\"348\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/05af38b19e0d23c7e4682728853b9425/tumblr_inline_nvte0ajzoN1tzhl5u_540.png\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"405\" data-orig-height=\"348\"/></figure><p>A company working with NASA invented the translucent ceramic that became the first <a href=\"http://ntrs.nasa.gov/archive/nasa/casi.ntrs.nasa.gov/20020087639.pdf\">invisible dental braces</a>, which would go on to become one of the best-selling orthodontic products of all time. </p>\n<hr><p>So, now that you know a few of the spinoff technologies that we helped develop, you can look for them throughout your day. Visit our page to learn about more spinoff technologies: <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/\">https://spinoff.nasa.gov</a></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br/></p>\n</blockquote>",
        "reblog": {

Airplane Wing Designs</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"720\" data-orig-height=\"540\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/24f05bfd8009e9e06440893fe89e75b0/tumblr_inline_nvtbeg25NZ1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"720\" data-orig-height=\"540\"></figure><p>Did you know that we’re with you when you fly? Key aerodynamic advances made by our researchers - such as the up-turned ends of wings, called “<a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2015/t_2.html\">winglets</a>” - are ubiquitous among modern aircraft and have saved many billions of dollars in fuel costs. </p>\n<p><b>4. Precision GPS</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"464\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/a3ae0be91feea51a3167bbf58792ab36/tumblr_inline_nvtbfwGT7H1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"464\"></figure><p>Uncorrected GPS data can be off by as much as 15 meters thanks to data errors, drift in satellite clocks and interference from Earth’s atmosphere. One of our software packages developed in the 1990s dials in these locations to within centimeters, enabling highly accurate GPS readings anywhere on the planet. One of our most important contributions to modern society, <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2010/ct_7.html\">precise GPS</a> is used in everything from personal devices and commercial airplanes to self-driving tractors. </p>\n<p><b>5. Memory Foam</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"300\" data-orig-height=\"243\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/ec21d3c52521b39163e2977aaa0ad28b/tumblr_inline_nvtbz1gG8b1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"300\" data-orig-height=\"243\"></figure><p>Possibly the most widely recognized spinoff, <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2005/ch_6.html\">memory foam</a> was invented by our researchers looking for ways to keep its test pilots and astronauts comfortable as they experienced extreme acceleration. Today, memory foam cushions beds, chairs, couches, car and motorcycle seats, shoes and even football helmets. </p>\n<p><b>6. International Search and Rescue System</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"350\" data-orig-height=\"308\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/9ac625142ced68c5b6a86ce266eae791/tumblr_inline_nvtcdx0eSx1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"350\" data-orig-height=\"308\"></figure><p>We pioneered the technology now used internationally for <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2012/ps_3.html\">search and rescue operations</a>. When pilots, sailors or other travelers and adventurers are stranded, they can activate a personal locator bacon that uses overhead satellites to relay their call for help and precise location to authorities. </p>\n<p><b>7. Improvements to Truck Aerodynamics</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"1362\" data-orig-height=\"1022\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/3ed08b3ff077ad6f11c1a62c0564001d/tumblr_inline_nvtcyfvNWf1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"1362\" data-orig-height=\"1022\"></figure><p>Nearly every truck on the road has been shaped by NASA - literally. Agency research in <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2008/t_3.html\">vehicle aerodynamic design</a> led to the curves and contours that help modern big rigs cut through the air with less drag. Our contributions to truck design have greatly reduced fuel consumption, perhaps by as much as 6,800 gallons per year for an average vehicle. </p>\n<p><b>8. Shock Absorbers for Buildings and Bridges</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"493\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/78fb9c082d319b39493373af2e70e76d/tumblr_inline_nvtd8oMjn71tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"493\"></figure><p><a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2015/ps_2.html\">Shock absorbers</a> originally designed to survive the extreme conditions of space shuttle launches are now bracing hundreds of buildings and bridges in earthquake-prone regions all over the world. None of which have suffered even minor damage during an earthquake. </p>\n<p><b>9. Advanced Water Filtration</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"570\" data-orig-height=\"411\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://36.media.tumblr.com/88b5702b695c048889103a6e4cf9443c/tumblr_inline_nvtdg86E4x1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"570\" data-orig-height=\"411\"></figure><p>We have recently discovered sources of water on the moon and Mars, but even so space is still practically a desert for human explorers, and every drop possible must be recycled and reused. A <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2013/cg_1.html\">nanofiber filer</a> devised to purify water in orbit is currently at work on Earth. From devices that supply water to remote villages, to a water bottle that lets hikers and adventurers stay hydrated using streams and lakes, our technology is being utilized. </p>\n<p><b>10. Invisible Braces</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"405\" data-orig-height=\"348\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/05af38b19e0d23c7e4682728853b9425/tumblr_inline_nvte0ajzoN1tzhl5u_540.png\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"405\" data-orig-height=\"348\"></figure><p>A company working with NASA invented the translucent ceramic that became the first <a href=\"http://ntrs.nasa.gov/archive/nasa/casi.ntrs.nasa.gov/20020087639.pdf\">invisible dental braces</a>, which would go on to become one of the best-selling orthodontic products of all time. </p>\n<hr>\n<p>So, now that you know a few of the spinoff technologies that we helped develop, you can look for them throughout your day. Visit our page to learn about more spinoff technologies: <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/\">https://spinoff.nasa.gov</a></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br></p>\n</blockquote>",

            "content_raw": "<p>What is a spinoff? Great question! A NASA spinoff is a technology, originally developed to meet our mission needs that has been transferred to the public and now provides benefits as a commercial product or service. Basically, we create awesome stuff and then share it with the world. Here’s a list of just a few NASA spinoff technologies (in no particular order): </p>\n<p><b>1. Enriched Baby Food</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"1300\" data-orig-height=\"945\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://40.media.tumblr.com/4673cedff12b548094a908f3a7df34db/tumblr_inline_nvtaxdjf2c1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"1300\" data-orig-height=\"945\"></figure><p>While developing life support for Mars missions, NASA-funded researchers discovered a natural source for an omega-3 fatty acid that plays a key role in infant development. The ingredient has since been infused in more than 99% of <a href=\"https://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2008/ch_8.html\">infant formula</a> on the market and is helping babies worldwide develop healthy brains, eyes and hearts. </p>\n<p><b>2. Digital Camera Sensors</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"490\" data-orig-height=\"277\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/15fc2651424f65612a728ae5ad2f646f/tumblr_inline_nvtb6u25681tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"490\" data-orig-height=\"277\"></figure><p>Whether you take pictures and videos with a DSLR camera, phone or even a GoPro, you’re using NASA technology. The <a href=\"http://spinoff.nasa.gov/Spinoff2010/cg_3.html\">CMOS active pixel sensor</a> in most digital image-capturing devices was invented when we needed to miniaturize cameras for interplanetary missions. </p>\n<p><b>3. Airplane Wing Designs</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"720\" data-orig-height=\"540\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://41.media.tumblr.com/24f05bfd8009e9e06440893fe89e75b0/tumblr_inline_nvtbeg25NZ1tzhl5u_540.jpg\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"720\" data-orig-height=\"540\"></figure><p>Did you know that we’re with you when you fly?       {
        "blog_name": "theverge",
        "id": 130814094364,
        "post_url": "http://theverge.tumblr.com/post/130814094364/whats-up-for-october",
        "slug": "whats-up-for-october",
        "type": "text",
        "date": "2015-10-09 14:22:19 GMT",
        "timestamp": 1444400539,
        "state": "published",
        "format": "html",
        "reblog_key": "Q5UIUytn",
        "tags": [],
        "short_url": "http://tmblr.co/ZP0mpw1vr8AmS",
        "summary": "What’s Up for October?",
        "recommended_source": null,
        "recommended_color": null,
        "followed": false,
        "highlighted": [],
        "liked": false,
        "note_count": 3378,
        "title": "What’s Up for October?",
        "body": "<p><a class=\"tumblr_blog\" href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/post/130279822574\">nasa</a>:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p><figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/0393060b84462133698a0d5302a4eb26/tumblr_inline_nvjwigWf1p1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure></p><p>This month is filled with <a href=\"https://youtu.be/DebUcfb0lmE\">exciting celestial sights</a>. Here are 10 targets you can view this month: </p>\n<p><b>10. Unusual Sunset</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/dcf9170acac83f4e9ebb834e1f36c7d1/tumblr_inline_nvjwrldbYD1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>During a sunset, our thick atmosphere absorbs most colors of sunlight, but red light is absorbed the least. Rarely, green flashes can be seen just above the sun’s edge. As the last sliver of the disk disappears below the horizon, be sure to watch its color. </p>\n<p><b>9. Belt of Venus</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/1e63afd10aa3573fabc846c61fc5cd2f/tumblr_inline_nvjwvnNfU01tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Just after sunset, turn around and face east. A dark shadow will move up from the horizon and gradually cover the pinkish sky. This is caused from the Earth itself blocking the sunlight and is called the Earth Shadow or the Belt of Venus. </p>\n<p><b>8. Crepuscular Rays</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/69ed0c07107d2a6ddbf35020a148dcfe/tumblr_inline_nvjx16YWJU1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Also just after sunset, or before dawn, you may see rays of sunlight spread like a fan. These are called crepuscular rays and are formed when sunlight streams through gaps in the clouds or mountains. </p>\n<p><b>7. Aurora Borealis</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/2f7ff870d7240d3c1918bb465d1e4185/tumblr_inline_nvjx6o3jw51tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>The northern lights, also known as the aurora borealis, are caused by collisions between gaseous particles in Earth’s atmosphere and charged particles released from the sun. The color of the lights can changed depending on the type of gas being struck by particles of solar wind. You can find out when and where to expect aurorae at the <a href=\"http://www.swpc.noaa.gov/\">Space Weather Prediction Center</a>. </p>\n<p><b>6. Andromeda Galaxy </b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/4a1afb7f06e5974f189dd6dbcdf08970/tumblr_inline_nvjxairYyF1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Did you now that The Andromeda Galaxy is one of the few you can actually see with your naked eye? In October, look nearly overhead after sunset to see it! This galaxy is more than twice the apparent width of the moon. </p>\n<p><b>5. Moon Features</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/3e7aec063326a65fee5053447a8cff78/tumblr_inline_nvjxdgB8Jl1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>Nights in mid-October are excellent for viewing the features on the moon. Areas like the Sea of Tranquility and the site of the 1969 Apollo 11 landing will be visible. </p>\n<p><b>4. A Comet </b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/ef3918bea8d63cc082fa9fda12a633c7/tumblr_inline_nvjxi66dmC1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>This month, the European Space Agency’s Rosetta mission target, a comet with a complicated name (Comet 67P Churyumov-Gerasimenko), is still bright enough for experienced astronomers to pick out in a dark sky. On October 9, you may be able to spot it in the east near the crescent moon and Venus. </p>\n<p><b>3. Meteor Showers</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/a4742eca19264905578f507da165fab8/tumblr_inline_nvjxloZAhB1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>There are multiple meteor showers this month. On the 9th: watch the faint, slow-moving Draconids. On the 10th: catch the slow, super-bright Taurids. And on the 21st: don’t’ miss the swift and bright Orionids from the dust of Comet Halley. </p>\n<p><b>2. Three Close Planets</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/1f3482111e66af56afd9adb85ef0a6c0/tumblr_inline_nvjxoxBbAG1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>On October 28, you’ll find a tight grouping of Jupiter, Venus and Mars in the eastern sky before sunrise. </p>\n<p><b>1. Zodiacal Light</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/357ce7bceb68ea7a0ff906b8493ad323/tumblr_inline_nvjxunHskP1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"/></figure><p>The Zodiacal light is a faint triangular glow that can be seen from a dark sky after sunset or before sunrise. What you’re seeing is sunlight reflecting off dust grains that circle the sun in the inner solar system. These dust grains travel in the same plane as the moon and planets as they journey across our sky.</p>\n<p>For more stargazing tools visit: <a href=\"http://solarsystem.nasa.gov/news/startoolbox\">Star Tool Box</a></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br/></p>\n</blockquote>",
        "reblog": {

A Comet </b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/ef3918bea8d63cc082fa9fda12a633c7/tumblr_inline_nvjxi66dmC1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>This month, the European Space Agency’s Rosetta mission target, a comet with a complicated name (Comet 67P Churyumov-Gerasimenko), is still bright enough for experienced astronomers to pick out in a dark sky. On October 9, you may be able to spot it in the east near the crescent moon and Venus. </p>\n<p><b>3. Meteor Showers</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/a4742eca19264905578f507da165fab8/tumblr_inline_nvjxloZAhB1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>There are multiple meteor showers this month. On the 9th: watch the faint, slow-moving Draconids. On the 10th: catch the slow, super-bright Taurids. And on the 21st: don’t’ miss the swift and bright Orionids from the dust of Comet Halley. </p>\n<p><b>2. Three Close Planets</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/1f3482111e66af56afd9adb85ef0a6c0/tumblr_inline_nvjxoxBbAG1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>On October 28, you’ll find a tight grouping of Jupiter, Venus and Mars in the eastern sky before sunrise. </p>\n<p><b>1. Zodiacal Light</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/357ce7bceb68ea7a0ff906b8493ad323/tumblr_inline_nvjxunHskP1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>The Zodiacal light is a faint triangular glow that can be seen from a dark sky after sunset or before sunrise. What you’re seeing is sunlight reflecting off dust grains that circle the sun in the inner solar system. These dust grains travel in the same plane as the moon and planets as they journey across our sky.</p>\n<p>For more stargazing tools visit: <a href=\"http://solarsystem.nasa.gov/news/startoolbox\">Star Tool Box</a></p>\n<p><i>Make sure to follow us on Tumblr for your regular dose of space: </i><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\"><i></i></a><a href=\"http://nasa.tumblr.com/\">http://nasa.tumblr.com</a><br></p>\n</blockquote>",

            "content_raw": "<p><figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/0393060b84462133698a0d5302a4eb26/tumblr_inline_nvjwigWf1p1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure></p>\n<p>This month is filled with <a href=\"https://youtu.be/DebUcfb0lmE\">exciting celestial sights</a>. Here are 10 targets you can view this month: </p>\n<p><b>10. Unusual Sunset</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/dcf9170acac83f4e9ebb834e1f36c7d1/tumblr_inline_nvjwrldbYD1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>During a sunset, our thick atmosphere absorbs most colors of sunlight, but red light is absorbed the least. Rarely, green flashes can be seen just above the sun’s edge. As the last sliver of the disk disappears below the horizon, be sure to watch its color. </p>\n<p><b>9. Belt of Venus</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/1e63afd10aa3573fabc846c61fc5cd2f/tumblr_inline_nvjwvnNfU01tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>Just after sunset, turn around and face east. A dark shadow will move up from the horizon and gradually cover the pinkish sky. This is caused from the Earth itself blocking the sunlight and is called the Earth Shadow or the Belt of Venus. </p>\n<p><b>8. Crepuscular Rays</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/69ed0c07107d2a6ddbf35020a148dcfe/tumblr_inline_nvjx16YWJU1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>Also just after sunset, or before dawn, you may see rays of sunlight spread like a fan. These are called crepuscular rays and are formed when sunlight streams through gaps in the clouds or mountains. </p>\n<p><b>7. Aurora Borealis</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/2f7ff870d7240d3c1918bb465d1e4185/tumblr_inline_nvjx6o3jw51tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>The northern lights, also known as the aurora borealis, are caused by collisions between gaseous particles in Earth’s atmosphere and charged particles released from the sun. The color of the lights can changed depending on the type of gas being struck by particles of solar wind. You can find out when and where to expect aurorae at the <a href=\"http://www.swpc.noaa.gov/\">Space Weather Prediction Center</a>. </p>\n<p><b>6. Andromeda Galaxy </b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/4a1afb7f06e5974f189dd6dbcdf08970/tumblr_inline_nvjxairYyF1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>Did you now that The Andromeda Galaxy is one of the few you can actually see with your naked eye? In October, look nearly overhead after sunset to see it! This galaxy is more than twice the apparent width of the moon. </p>\n<p><b>5. Moon Features</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/3e7aec063326a65fee5053447a8cff78/tumblr_inline_nvjxdgB8Jl1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>Nights in mid-October are excellent for viewing the features on the moon. Areas like the Sea of Tranquility and the site of the 1969 Apollo 11 landing will be visible. </p>\n<p><b>4. A Comet </b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/ef3918bea8d63cc082fa9fda12a633c7/tumblr_inline_nvjxi66dmC1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>This month, the European Space Agency’s Rosetta mission target, a comet with a complicated name (Comet 67P Churyumov-Gerasimenko), is still bright enough for experienced astronomers to pick out in a dark sky. On October 9, you may be able to spot it in the east near the crescent moon and Venus. </p>\n<p><b>3. Meteor Showers</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://31.media.tumblr.com/a4742eca19264905578f507da165fab8/tumblr_inline_nvjxloZAhB1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>There are multiple meteor showers this month. On the 9th: watch the faint, slow-moving Draconids. On the 10th: catch the slow, super-bright Taurids. And on the 21st: don’t’ miss the swift and bright Orionids from the dust of Comet Halley. </p>\n<p><b>2. Three Close Planets</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://38.media.tumblr.com/1f3482111e66af56afd9adb85ef0a6c0/tumblr_inline_nvjxoxBbAG1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>On October 28, you’ll find a tight grouping of Jupiter, Venus and Mars in the eastern sky before sunrise. </p>\n<p><b>1. Zodiacal Light</b></p>\n<figure data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\" class=\"tmblr-full\"><img src=\"http://33.media.tumblr.com/357ce7bceb68ea7a0ff906b8493ad323/tumblr_inline_nvjxunHskP1tzhl5u_500.gif\" alt=\"image\" data-orig-width=\"400\" data-orig-height=\"225\"></figure><p>The Zodiacal light is a faint triangular glow that can be seen from a dark sky after sunset or before sunrise.         "post_url": "http://theverge.tumblr.com/post/130586487009/the-martian-movie-and-our-real-journey-to-mars",
